<div id="review_2537617939" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2537617939" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2537617939?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=mathematics">mathematics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=baseball">baseball</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=disabilities">disabilities</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2537617939" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5938118409881272677">This tale charms us with the friendship that develops between a young housekeeper and her 12-year old son with an elderly recluse and former mathematics professor. Though he can‚Äôt retain new memories beyond 80 minutes, his 50-plus years of skills developed as a mathematician prior to the accident and brain damage that disabled him are still accessible to him. These skills he creatively puts to use each day trying to figure out and live in whatever social world of people falls his way as if anew.</span>
  <span id="freeText5938118409881272677" style="display:none">This tale charms us with the friendship that develops between a young housekeeper and her 12-year old son with an elderly recluse and former mathematics professor. Though he can‚Äôt retain new memories beyond 80 minutes, his 50-plus years of skills developed as a mathematician prior to the accident and brain damage that disabled him are still accessible to him. These skills he creatively puts to use each day trying to figure out and live in whatever social world of people falls his way as if anew. The ‚ÄúHousekeeper‚Äù (the only name we have for our narrator) learns how to promote continuity with the self-absorbed and fragile octogenarian by pinning to his shirt to see each morning a drawing of herself and a square-root symbol for her son (who becomes ‚ÄúRoot‚Äù because of his flat head). It felt brilliant how Ogawa‚Äôs three characters formed a fruitful virtual family despite all the challenges of the old man‚Äôs memory problems.<br /><br />The Professor has outlived any friends and family he might have had, and his one limited family member is a daughter-in-law who provides his apartment and hires housekeepers told to handle all problems themselves. Thus, the Housekeeper understands how she is his only human connection, and her kind nature leads her open whatever doors to his heart can be opened. His main occupation during the day is solving math puzzles posed by certain journals, a task that can build each day on his own written proofs and series of equations from the day before. His use of math as a timeless way to query and grasp the world becomes quite a revelation to her. She even gets inspired enough to work on math puzzles herself during pauses in her day, driven by the sense of wonderful truths about reality are there to be discovered. <br /><br />The reader needs no facility with math to appreciate the hold of mathematical thinking on the characters (i.e. as was true of ‚ÄúThe Curious Case of the Dog in the Nighttime‚Äù and ‚ÄúThe Beautiful Mind‚Äù). For example, The Professor likes to ask the Housekeeper what her birthday is or some other quantity question and then identify amazing mathematical properties of the number, such as it being a ‚ÄúPerfect Number‚Äù (one equal to the sum of its factors) or a special kind of Prime number (one that has no factors but one and itself). The Professor takes special pleasure in treating her son to tales of the wonders of numbers, such as the weird features of Imaginary numbers (one based on the square root of a negative number). The Housekeeper breaks the rules of her agency by bringing Root to her job each day after his school, as the pleasures of her son‚Äôs bond with the Professor clearly enhances the quality of both their lives. The fatherless boy gets beneficent wisdom from an adult stand-in and learns the empathetic ability to protect him from the pain of learning out of synch his memories are with the present world. For example, they share the common pleasures of a favorite baseball team‚Äôs successes, but Root makes up a story for why the Professor‚Äôs hero of the 50s is not in the games they share. <br /><br />A read of this book provides some of the same rewards of cross-generational friendships and collisions of the serious and the comic that are common in the work of Fred Backman. On top of the fuzzy warmth of experiencing the breaking down of barriers between people, this novel adds an artful layer of mathematics played out as a language that can mediate the kind of human connections and framework for reality that all of us need to fully live in this lonely life.<br /> <br />As a sample of the frequent zest of math applied to life, here the Housekeeper is tending the Professor when he is sick and she is realizing how fragile he is:<br /><i>I remembered something the Professor told me, something a mathematician with a difficult name once said: ‚ÄúMath has proven the existence of God, because it is absolute and without contradiction; but the devil must exist as well, because we cannot prove it.‚Äù The Professor‚Äôs body had been consumed by the devil of mathematics.</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5938118409881272677" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10278094193264536592"><b> 3.5 Star</b><br/><br/>The Housekeeper + The Professor by Yoko Ogawa is a heart-warming story about a housekeeper, a professor and the housekeeper‚Äôs ten year old son but also and most importantly a story about what it means to live in the present, and about friendships, family and respect and a little Math thrown in for good measure.<br/><br/>"He is a brilliant maths professor who lives with only eighty minutes of short-term memory. She is a sensitive and astute young housekeeper who is entrusted to take care of him" <br/><br/>T</span>
  <span id="freeText10278094193264536592" style="display:none"><b> 3.5 Star</b><br /><br />The Housekeeper + The Professor by Yoko Ogawa is a heart-warming story about a housekeeper, a professor and the housekeeper‚Äôs ten year old son but also and most importantly a story about what it means to live in the present, and about friendships, family and respect and a little Math thrown in for good measure.<br /><br />"He is a brilliant maths professor who lives with only eighty minutes of short-term memory. She is a sensitive and astute young housekeeper who is entrusted to take care of him" <br /><br />The premise for this novel is very simple a young women is assigned to a job caring for an elderly, brain damaged professor of mathematics. He has only eighty minutes of short-term memory, so he doesn't remember her from one day to the next, but his memory pre his accident of 1975 remains intact. He continues to function and live his life from day to day with the help of his housekeeper, his notes and most importantly his love of numbers. The story is narrated by the housekeeper.<br /><br />I did enjoy this story and came to really like the unique characters and quirky story of this book. While it is a quick read it has a slow release plot and a story that your will read with care and perhaps re-read some chapters twice.<br /><br />However I was disappointed a little in the sense of time and location of the novel. The novel is set in Japan but for me I never got the sense of location and this was a disappointment in the story. I really need the scene to be set and the little details and place descriptions are as important to me as the plot.<br /><br />I think this would make a good book club read as there is so much to discuss about this story. <br />I enjoyed the story and especially the characters.<br /></span>

  <span id="freeText227572039428522857" style="display:none">This is a quietly wonderful book. When I was reading it I really liked it. Now that some time has passed I still think of it, and any book that I still remember months after reading is a book that deserves 5 stars. Although there is some advanced math in the story the author doesn't expect the reader to understand all of it. The numbers and math are used more to show the magic of numbers and how math brings an unlikely group of people together to form meaningful relationships. <br />This is a character driven book, and Ogawa does a beautiful job of fleshing out all three characters and making them real. This is a book that will linger with the reader long after the last page is turned. </span>

<div id="review_2570173195" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2570173195" />
    <a title="Rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3965659-rachel"><img alt="Rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595877858p2/3965659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2570173195?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/3965659-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3965659-rachel?shelf=east-asia">east-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3965659-rachel?shelf=in-translation">in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3965659-rachel?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3965659-rachel?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3965659-rachel?shelf=novellas">novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3965659-rachel?shelf=wit">wit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2570173195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10347352682123767673">My only experience with Yoko Ogawa before now was her terrific short story collection <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16032127.Revenge' title='Revenge by Y≈çko Ogawa' rel='nofollow noopener'>Revenge</a>, and though I'd heard that all of her books are drastically different from one another, I think I still expected to see a bit of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16032127.Revenge' title='Revenge by Y≈çko Ogawa' rel='nofollow noopener'>Revenge</a>'s dark and macabre tone here. Instead, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3181564.The_Housekeeper_and_the_Professor' title='The Housekeeper and the Professor by Y≈çko Ogawa' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Housekeeper and the Professor</a> is utterly and unapologetically charming.<br/><br/>It focuses on the relationship between a housekeeper, her son, and a man whose house she's assigned to by her agency, who she refers to only as 'the Profes</span>
  <span id="freeText10347352682123767673" style="display:none">My only experience with Yoko Ogawa before now was her terrific short story collection <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16032127.Revenge" title="Revenge by Y≈çko Ogawa" rel="nofollow noopener">Revenge</a>, and though I'd heard that all of her books are drastically different from one another, I think I still expected to see a bit of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16032127.Revenge" title="Revenge by Y≈çko Ogawa" rel="nofollow noopener">Revenge</a>'s dark and macabre tone here. Instead, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3181564.The_Housekeeper_and_the_Professor" title="The Housekeeper and the Professor by Y≈çko Ogawa" rel="nofollow noopener">The Housekeeper and the Professor</a> is utterly and unapologetically charming.<br /><br />It focuses on the relationship between a housekeeper, her son, and a man whose house she's assigned to by her agency, who she refers to only as 'the Professor.' The Professor has a condition which prevents him from creating new memories - his memory lasts only 80 minutes. He is nevertheless a brilliant man, and the likewise unnamed protagonist becomes increasingly spellbound by his unsolicited mathematical lectures.<br /><br />There are any number of reasons why this book shouldn't have worked for me; it's sweet in tone, it's about math, it's about baseball. I don't like any of these things. However, this book's passion is positively <i>infectious</i>; this was like listening to a friend tell a story about something they love which you don't particularly care about - sometimes regardless of the content, the enthusiasm itself is contagious. Also, I will say that this book helped me understand the sentiment that 'math is like poetry,' that I've heard a few times throughout my life, better than anything else ever has. No matter how many times I've witnessed an individual's passion for math, it's always seemed to me like this cold and rigid thing, but The Professor's perspective on the relationship between numbers, and the solace he takes in their familiarity, really moved me.<br /><br />But beyond the math and baseball, what makes this quirky book near-universally appealing is the unconventional, infinitely touching relationship between the two titular characters. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3181564.The_Housekeeper_and_the_Professor" title="The Housekeeper and the Professor by Y≈çko Ogawa" rel="nofollow noopener">The Housekeeper and the Professor</a> is about empathy; it's a testament to the unexpected possibilities of human connection. I just found this book to be a joy to read - quiet and subtle and nostalgic and affecting. Very highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10347352682123767673" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Connie G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g"><img alt="Connie G" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307502493p2/5364077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194514346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3827529176669414990">This is a lovely story where kindness turns unrelated people into a family. The Housekeeper is hired to cook and keep house for a Professor of mathematics who had a traumatic brain injury after a car accident. Although the Professor can remember events that happened before the TBI, this injury resulted in his short-term memory lasting only eighty minutes. It seems like they are meeting for the first time every day. <br/><br/>The Housekeeper and her ten year old son interact quite well with the professor s</span>
  <span id="freeText3827529176669414990" style="display:none">This is a lovely story where kindness turns unrelated people into a family. The Housekeeper is hired to cook and keep house for a Professor of mathematics who had a traumatic brain injury after a car accident. Although the Professor can remember events that happened before the TBI, this injury resulted in his short-term memory lasting only eighty minutes. It seems like they are meeting for the first time every day. <br /><br />The Housekeeper and her ten year old son interact quite well with the professor since they all are enthusiastic about baseball which has lots of mathematical statistics. The Professor loves children, and enjoys sharing his knowledge of the beauty of mathematics while bringing the subject down to their level. The Housekeeper and her son were adept at making a personal connection with the Professor, even if he could not remember it the next day. I enjoyed this charming, heartwarming story.<br /><br />First read Oct 2011. Reread March 2018.</span>

<div id="review_54446407" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/54446407" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/54446407?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=kirkus">kirkus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=text-checked">text-checked</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer54446407" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5956258052552424123">This book is truly original, not your normal run of the mill. I highly recommend it. What is it about? It is about friendship and the beauty of numbers and baseball. And more..... Where should I start? I will start with the numbers. This is sort of the easiest to expalin. This book made me see and feel the beauty of math, of the laws that govern numbers. The world is so complicated. We understand nothing. Everything is always changing, but then you learn of a mathematical formula that is constan</span>
  <span id="freeText5956258052552424123" style="display:none">This book is truly original, not your normal run of the mill. I highly recommend it. What is it about? It is about friendship and the beauty of numbers and baseball. And more..... Where should I start? I will start with the numbers. This is sort of the easiest to expalin. This book made me see and feel the beauty of math, of the laws that govern numbers. The world is so complicated. We understand nothing. Everything is always changing, but then you learn of a mathematical formula that is constant, that is always true, and suddenly there is an order to the messy world around us. The author goes even one step beyond this. Three people, of which two are completely normal like you and me, the housekeeper and her son, come to understand the magic of numbers through their contact with the Professor (of math). I wish I had had had a math teacher like te Professor! Did you know that zero is a concept thought up by an Indian mathematician. Before him the Greeks thought this concept didn't need a symbol. You get history, and then the author pulls the mathematical concept into the story so you understand the true consequence of the concept. What it means to you and me. Infinity, a line, zero, amicable numbers. Honestly, math is made beautiful. Occasionaly, I was too tired to follow the full development of a formula, but usually the author never pushed the theoretical underpinings too far. It is definitely worth reading the book just so that you will learn about math, but there is more! <br /><br />There is baseball. And there is true friendship. And there is a look at how people come to love each other simply by doing things together. Caring for each other. All the blurbs about this book tell you that the housekeeper is taking care of an elderly man who can only remember what has happened during the last 80 minutes. He can also remember everything before 1975, before a car accident. What does this lack if remembrance really mean? It means that every day when she and her son come to his door he doesn't know who they are. It means that when you spend a wonderful afternoon together, it is gone tomorrow. So are the frightful disagreements that can destroy a day. Gone - they never happened! What does this say to the reader? What's the lesson to be learned? You get a beautiful picture of a frail old man, who has three suits - one for winter, one for summer and one for the spring and fall. That is what he wears. He has little pieces of paper pinned all over his suit to remind himeslf of things he must remember. Soon he is not a man dressed in a suit, but a man dressed in fragments of paper. The image is beautiful and clear and wonderful. Why? Bcause you fall in love with the guy! What develops for realationship between these three people is extraordinary. Do you see why I say this book is original? It is NOT melodramatic, it is quiet and beautiful. Heartwarming - definitely. Read it. It's only 180 pages. Take time to read chapter 10, of the eleven chapters in the book, slowly. Savour it. It is so beautiful. <br /><br />12 pages in and I already love the characters, the housekeeper and the professor! Why is that? Perhaps b/c they are each true to their own selves. They are not what society wants them to be but merely who they were born to be. I cannot help but compare this with the poor souls of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6457081.Remarkable_Creatures_" title="Remarkable Creatures  by Tracy Chevalier" rel="nofollow noopener">Remarkable Creatures</a>. Although both Elizabeth and Mary also followed their inclinations, they spent all their time judging themselves by the moral standards of their times. The housekeeper and the professor are too busy being themselves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5956258052552424123" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Even numbers.<br />And her prose, oh my goodness, what a joy to read; Gourmet treats for the brain, literally.<br /><br />On the surface there‚Äôs not a lot happening in this little gem. <div id="review_1189627522" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1189627522" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1189627522?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1189627522" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13365457218760492242"><b>3.5</b><br/><br/>After our first full day visiting Japan in 2011, I saw a baseball game on TV (had to be a replay because it was December): the Yakult Swallows versus the Hanshin Tigers. I recognized Matt Murton of the Tigers who'd briefly played for the Chicago Cubs and the name of the 40-year-old Miyamoto. Two evenings later, in Nara, we got caught up in a small parade on the main street and as it ended, a young man straddling a bicycle caught up with us to ask if we were Americans and, next, if we knew Ame</span>
  <span id="freeText13365457218760492242" style="display:none"><b>3.5</b><br /><br />After our first full day visiting Japan in 2011, I saw a baseball game on TV (had to be a replay because it was December): the Yakult Swallows versus the Hanshin Tigers. I recognized Matt Murton of the Tigers who'd briefly played for the Chicago Cubs and the name of the 40-year-old Miyamoto. Two evenings later, in Nara, we got caught up in a small parade on the main street and as it ended, a young man straddling a bicycle caught up with us to ask if we were Americans and, next, if we knew American baseball. When I asked if he knew American baseball, his response was "of course". (I soon understood his "of course" simply meant "yes".) His favorite Japanese team was the Hanshin Tigers, so I mentioned Murton, and he gave some other names of Americans playing in Japan. I mentioned Fukudome* (who was then playing for the Cubs) and he countered with Sammy Sosa! The young man walked with us to the hotel where we said good-bye, and he seemed reluctant to part. <br /><br />I bring this up not only because in this book the Hanshin Tigers are the favorite team of the professor and the son of the housekeeper; but because baseball (along with mathematics) is the way the housekeeper and her son communicate with the professor. I don't speak Japanese and the young man's English was limited, yet we communicated -- and quite satisfactorily -- through the names of baseball players.<br /><br />This book is very different from the other works of Ogawa that have been translated into English. Anyone reading this first and then the others might be in for a shock. And while I prefer her <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16032127.Revenge" title="Revenge by Y≈çko Ogawa" rel="nofollow noopener">Revenge</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1337973.The_Diving_Pool_Three_Novellas" title="The Diving Pool Three Novellas by Y≈çko Ogawa" rel="nofollow noopener">The Diving Pool</a>, her narratorial choice here (as usual) is intriguing. The housekeeper tells us nothing of how hard and lonely her own life must be, as if to point out how little she knows of the professor's feelings. She never questions the giving of friendship to a man who will have no memory of it eighty minutes later. The professor may live in a sort of bubble, but surely the housekeeper doesn't, though that is what she presents to us. Because of these choices, on the surface the story might seem mostly a sweet one (and it is, though it's never saccharine) but I can't help sense the sadness that lurks beneath.<br /><br />* I googled Fukudome to see where he is playing now, and had to smile ... the Hanshin Tigers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13365457218760492242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_496476714" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/496476714" />
    <a title="Ashley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/737133-ashley"><img alt="Ashley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365357522p2/737133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/496476714?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley" href="/user/show/737133-ashley">Ashley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ashley by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/185835-yulia">Yulia</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/737133-ashley?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/737133-ashley?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer496476714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10137202853574322556">I‚Äôm not sure on which page I fell in love with the wrinkled, snowy-haired Professor in his rumpled suit with the scrap-paper reminders pinned all over it (the most important one being the note that reads ‚ÄúMy memory lasts only eighty minutes‚Äù). I just know that about 50 pages from the end of the story, I found myself digging in my heels, wanting to linger over every last word. <i>The Housekeeper and the Professor</i> is a gently written, beautiful musing on the nature of friendship and familial love. An</span>
  <span id="freeText10137202853574322556" style="display:none">I‚Äôm not sure on which page I fell in love with the wrinkled, snowy-haired Professor in his rumpled suit with the scrap-paper reminders pinned all over it (the most important one being the note that reads ‚ÄúMy memory lasts only eighty minutes‚Äù). I just know that about 50 pages from the end of the story, I found myself digging in my heels, wanting to linger over every last word. <i>The Housekeeper and the Professor</i> is a gently written, beautiful musing on the nature of friendship and familial love. And it‚Äôs a peek into a brilliant mind‚Äîone that sees the entire visible world in terms of the mathematics that underpins it; one that effortlessly finds a connection between his new housekeeper‚Äôs birth date and the three-digit number etched on the back of his wristwatch; one that is most alive in the world of formulas and theorems. (Several formulas appear in the narrative, but as someone who is definitely not a math person, let me assure you that you don‚Äôt have to be into numbers to appreciate the story. And you can always let your eyes glaze over a bit on the math if you want‚Äîyou won‚Äôt miss anything crucial.) <br /><br />With all of the little torn bits of paper I used to mark my favorite passages, before long my copy of the book started to look something like the Professor‚Äôs suit. I‚Äôll leave you with a handful of them:<br /><blockquote><i>‚ÄúI uncovered propositions that existed out there long before we were born. It‚Äôs like copying truths from God‚Äôs notebook, though we aren‚Äôt always sure where to find this notebook or when it will be open.‚Äù ‚Ä¶ The Professor picked up a branch and began to scratch something in the dirt. ... The withered stick gracefully carved</i> [<i>his</i>]<i> thoughts into the dry earth, and before long the lacy pattern of the formula was spread out at our feet.</i> <br />(Possibly I like this passage so much because it reminds me of another Man I love who once stooped to write mysteries in the dirt.)<br /><br /><i>He treated</i> [<i>my son</i>]<i> exactly as he treated prime numbers. For him, primes were the base on which all other natural numbers relied; and children were the foundation of everything worthwhile in the adult world.<br /><br />In my imagination, I saw the creator of the universe sitting in some distant corner of the sky, weaving a pattern of delicate lace so fine that even the faintest light would shine through it. The lace stretches out infinitely in every direction, billowing gently in the cosmic breeze. You want desperately to touch it, hold it up to the light, rub it against your cheek. And all we ask is to be able to re-create the pattern, weave it again with numbers, somehow, in our own language; to make even the tiniest fragment our own, to bring it back to earth.</i></blockquote> And maybe the best description of a thunderstorm I‚Äôve ever read:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>The trees suddenly began to tremble and the sky grew dark. The line of hills on the horizon, faintly visible just a moment before, disappeared in the gloom. There was a rumbling in the distance. <br /><br />‚ÄúThunder!‚Äù we said together, as the rain began to fall in enormous drops. The pounding on the roof echoed through the room. I stood up to close the windows, but the Professor stopped me. <br /><br />‚ÄúLeave them,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúIt feels good to have them open.‚Äù<br /><br />The curtains billowed in the breeze, letting the rain pour in on our bare feet. It was cool and refreshing, just as the Professor had said. The sun had vanished and the only light in the garden was the faint glow from the lamp above the kitchen sink. Small birds flitted among the drooping, tangled branches of the trees, and then the rain obscured everything.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="10137202853574322556" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2118653697?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2118653697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3036931588813664819">Imagine going to work and having to introduce yourself afresh to your employer every day. Will it drive you crazy if the first question you had to answer at the door each day is ‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs your shoe size?‚Äù ?<br/><br/>A housekeeper, a single mother with a 10-year-old son, finds herself at the cottage of a 64-year-old Math professor who has had nine housekeepers before her. She expects a difficult client, but what she least expects is an affection that develops into a strong friendship.<br/><br/>The Professor suffered a</span>
  <span id="freeText3036931588813664819" style="display:none">Imagine going to work and having to introduce yourself afresh to your employer every day. Will it drive you crazy if the first question you had to answer at the door each day is ‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs your shoe size?‚Äù ?<br /><br />A housekeeper, a single mother with a 10-year-old son, finds herself at the cottage of a 64-year-old Math professor who has had nine housekeepers before her. She expects a difficult client, but what she least expects is an affection that develops into a strong friendship.<br /><br />The Professor suffered a traumatic brain injury in a car accident 17 years ago in 1975 that left his memory in shreds. He is unable to remember anything new and his memory lasts precisely 80 minutes. Mercifully, he lives in the world of numbers and that remains intact. I cannot fathom what it must be like to wake up each day and have to start all over again to make sense of the environment. Ogawa tells us that "‚Ä¶he talked about numbers whenever he was unsure of what to say or do. Numbers were also his way of reaching out to the world. They were safe, a source of comfort." He does not remember the housekeeper from one day to the next. However, they can always discuss one thing without worry ‚Äì Mathematics.<br /><br />What I found loveliest is the gradual and steady flowering of the relationship between the Professor, the housekeeper, and her son, Root (a name fondly given by the Professor who delights in mussing the hair on the boy‚Äôs flat head). The Professor tells Root, "The square root sign is a generous symbol, it gives shelter to all the numbers." How wonderful!<br /><br />The housekeeper goes beyond the call of duty to make the Professor‚Äôs daily living as stable as possible. She enlarges his world beyond the office where he does his "thinking". The Professor imparts to the housekeeper, Root, and me (the reluctant Math student) the Mathematical secrets hidden in ‚Äúgod‚Äôs notebook‚Äù. I learned about amicable numbers, perfect numbers, and Euler‚Äôs formula. To the Professor, "..explaining why a formula is beautiful is like trying to explain why stars are beautiful." He teaches this beauty with incredible patience, enthusiasm, and humility. I found delight in this Math palindrome ‚Äì ‚ÄúI prefer pi‚Äù. I would have loved a Math teacher/professor like him. But what moved me most is the Professor‚Äôs genuine love for Root that is eloquently expressed despite his frailty and vulnerability.<br /><br />Read <b>The Housekeeper and the Professor</b>. How immeasurably priceless it is that a person may lose his brain and memory and yet keep his heart and compassion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3036931588813664819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText4105042236073611614" style="display:none">This was a delightful story about a single mother, who was also a professional housekeeper and her 10 year old son Root, befriending her new employer, a retired math professor, who due to a tragic accident is left with the ability to only retain information for eighty minutes, before it vanishes from his memory. <span id="freeTextContainer18356857431450715838">Sometime in high school I decided I didn't like and couldn't learn math. Also, I had no interest in sports. Until reading this elegant little book about a mathematics professor, his housekeeper, and her young baseball-loving son, I never realized the beauty I have denied myself.<br/><br/>Because I don't even remember the math I learned and because I have never followed baseball, I missed the poetry of this book, even as I sense it's there.   <span id="freeText18356857431450715838" style="display:none">Sometime in high school I decided I didn't like and couldn't learn math. Also, I had no interest in sports. Until reading this elegant little book about a mathematics professor, his housekeeper, and her young baseball-loving son, I never realized the beauty I have denied myself.<br /><br />Because I don't even remember the math I learned and because I have never followed baseball, I missed the poetry of this book, even as I sense it's there. I know it's there because of lines like this: <blockquote>"Eternal truths are ultimately invisible, and you won't find them in material things or natural phenomena, or even in human emotions. Mathematics, however, can illuminate them, can give them expression‚Äîin fact, nothing can prevent it from doing so." (116)<br /><br />or<br /><br />e [superscript]œÄ i +1 = 0 <br />I looked at the Professor's note again. A number that cycled on forever and another vague figure that never revealed its true nature now traced a short and elegant trajectory to a single point. Though there was no circle in evidence,<i> œÄ</i> had descended from somewhere to join hands with <i>e</i>. There they rested, slumped against each other, and it only remained for a human being to add 1, and the world suddenly changed. Everything resolved into nothing, zero. (127)</blockquote>I can <i>feel</i> the poetry, but understand none of it in an intellectual way‚Äîwhich is both exhilarating and painful.<br /><br /><b>11/18/20 Note</b><br />Thank you, BlackOxford aka Michael for articulating in your wonderful review what I felt but could not explain. For anyone who similarly wants to understand this book on a math level, read his review at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/190450590">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9139531476886166031">I'm a poet, not a mathematician, but I know exactly what you mean here, Betsy.. When I was in my mid-30s, I took a special geometry class (I wish I co</span>
  <span id="freeText9139531476886166031" style="display:none">I'm a poet, not a mathematician, but I know exactly what you mean here, Betsy.. When I was in my mid-30s, I took a special geometry class (I wish I could remember what it was called, but I can't), and none of it was on paper. The teacher used all of our bodies, positioned us in different parts of the room, and had us move to different positions. At the end of the class she explained what was happening when we were in such and such position and all of a sudden a subject that had eluded me my entire life made sense to me, and it was beautiful! I have struggled with a poor grasp of spatial/mechanical concepts, and standard geometry in the 9th grade almost ruined my GPA, and my struggles with it caused me to fail a college math class as well. If only I could have had the different approach to geometry back then. It's amazing what progressive teachers and research can do for us.</span>

        <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raul Bimenyimana" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana">Raul Bimenyimana</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3040212568" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17461216210857730872">The title of the book sums up what this story is about, a housekeeper who is employed to take care of a cottage belonging to a mathematics professor who, because of an accident, has a memory that lasts eighty minutes, and the housekeeper's son named root by the professor, because the shape of his head resembles that of a square root. While doing her work, the housekeeper and her son develop a strong friendship with the professor and are connected to each other by, you guessed it - mathematics - </span>
  <span id="freeText17461216210857730872" style="display:none">The title of the book sums up what this story is about, a housekeeper who is employed to take care of a cottage belonging to a mathematics professor who, because of an accident, has a memory that lasts eighty minutes, and the housekeeper's son named root by the professor, because the shape of his head resembles that of a square root. While doing her work, the housekeeper and her son develop a strong friendship with the professor and are connected to each other by, you guessed it - mathematics - and baseball.<br /><br />On the surface, this sounds like a boring story and while reading you expect "something" to happen, an event that is cataclysmic, that would push the story, but the event never really arrives, not the way I'd expect it to at least, yet the story still moves, entrancing till the end. This is a quiet book, wonderfully written, about human connections, friendship, and the beauty of numbers and sometimes it is just the kind of book you need to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17461216210857730872" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return

<span id="freeTextContainer3597862460257886798">Y≈çko <b>Ogawa</b> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9787965' rel='nofollow noopener'>Â∞èÂ∑ù Ê¥ãÂ≠ê</a>) was born in Okayama, Okayama Prefecture, graduated from Waseda University, and lives in Ashiya. Since 1988, she has published more than twenty works of fiction and nonfiction. Her novel <i>The Professor and his Beloved Equation</i> has been made into a movie.   <span id="freeText3597862460257886798" style="display:none">Y≈çko <b>Ogawa</b> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9787965" rel="nofollow noopener">Â∞èÂ∑ù Ê¥ãÂ≠ê</a>) was born in Okayama, Okayama Prefecture, graduated from Waseda University, and lives in Ashiya. Since 1988, she has published more than twenty works of fiction and nonfiction. Her novel <i>The Professor and his Beloved Equation</i> has been made into a movie. In 2006 she co-authored ‚ÄûAn Introduction to the World's Most Elegant Mathematics‚Äú with Masahiko Fujiwara, a mathematician, as a dialogue on the extraordinary beauty of numbers.<br /><br />A film in French, ‚ÄûL'Annulaire‚Äú (The Ringfinger), directed by Diane Bertrand, starring Olga Kurylenko and Marc Barb√©, was released in France in June 2005 and subsequently made the rounds of the international film festivals; the film, some of which is filmed in the Hamburg docks, is based in part on Ogawa's ‚ÄûKusuriyubi no hy≈çhon‚Äú (Ëñ¨Êåá„ÅÆÊ®ôÊú¨), translated into French as ‚ÄûL'Annulaire‚Äú (by Rose-Marie Makino-Fayolle who has translated numerous works by Ogawa, as well as works by Akira Yoshimura and by Ranpo Edogawa, into French).<br /><br />Kenzabur≈ç ≈åe has said, 'Y≈çko Ogawa is able to give expression to the most subtle workings of human psychology in prose that is gentle yet penetrating.' The subtlety in part lies in the fact that Ogawa's characters often seem not to know why they are doing what they are doing. She works by accumulation of detail, a technique that is perhaps more successful in her shorter works; the slow pace of development in the longer works requires something of a deus ex machina to end them. The reader is presented with an acute description of what the protagonists, mostly but not always female, observe and feel and their somewhat alienated self-observations, some of which is a reflection of Japanese society and especially women's roles within in it. The tone of her works varies, across the works and sometimes within the longer works, from the surreal, through the grotesque and the--sometimes grotesquely--humorous, to the psychologically ambiguous and even disturbing.</span>
